Department of Astronomy, Graduate School of Science, University of Tokyo 7-3-1, Hongo Bunkyo-ku, Tokyo 113-0033, Japan ; Department of Astronomical Science, The Graduate University for Advanced Studies (SOKENDAI), National Astronomical Observatory of Japan, 2-21-1,Osawa, Mitaka, Tokyo 181-8588, Japan), AD(Department of Physics, Nara National College of Technology, Yamatokohriyama, Nara 639-1080, Japan), AE(Department of Physics, Toho University, Funabashi, Chiba 274-8510, Japan), AF(Department of Astronomy, University of Michigan, 500 Church Street, Ann Arbor, MI 48109-1042, USA), AG(Argelander-Institut f\u00fcr Astronomie, Auf dem H\u00fcgel 71, D-53121 Bonn, Germany), AH(Princeton University Observatory, Peyton Hall, Princeton, NJ 08544, USA)"], "journal_name": "The Astrophysical Journal", "volume": "765", "publication_year": "2013/03/1", "start_page": "139", "keywords": ["gravitational lensing: strong", "quasars: individual: SDSS J132059.17+164402.59 SDSS J132059.73+164405.6", "Astrophysics - Cosmology and Nongalactic Astrophysics"], "url": "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2013ApJ...765..139R", "abstract": "We report the discovery of a pair of quasars at z = 1.487, with a separation of 8.''585 \u00b1 0.''002. Subaru Telescope infrared imaging reveals the presence of an elliptical and a disk-like galaxy located almost symmetrically between the quasars, in a cross-like configuration. Based on absorption lines in the quasar spectra and the colors of the galaxies, we estimate that both galaxies are located at redshift z = 0.899. This, as well as the similarity of the quasar spectra, suggests that the system is a single quasar multiply imaged by a galaxy group or cluster acting as a gravitational lens, although the possibility of a binary quasar cannot be fully excluded. We show that the gravitational lensing hypothesis implies that these galaxies are not isolated, but must be embedded in a dark matter halo of virial mass ~4 \u00d7 10<SUP>14</SUP> h <SUP>-1</SUP> <SUB>70</SUB> M <SUB>\u2609</SUB> assuming a Navarro-Frenk-White model with a concentration parameter of c <SUB>vir</SUB> = 6, or a singular isothermal sphere profile with a velocity dispersion of ~670 km s<SUP>-1</SUP>. We place constraints on the location of the dark matter halo, as well as the velocity dispersions of the galaxies. In addition, we discuss the influence of differential reddening, microlensing, and intrinsic variability on the quasar spectra and broadband photometry. <P />Based on data collected at Subaru Telescope, which is operated by the National Astronomical Observatory of Japan. Use of the UH2.2 m telescope for the observations is supported by NAOJ.", "doi": "10.1088/0004-637X/765/2/139", "custom1": "eprint: arXiv:1206.2011", "issn": "0004-637X", "number": "2"}, "Paper text": <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">THE QUASAR-GALAXY CROSS SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644: A PROBABLE LARGE-SEPARATION LENSED QUASAR<SUP>*</SUP>       </h1>\n\n<p class=\"mb-0\">\n\t<span data-authors=\"\">\n\t\t<span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Cristian E. class=\"icon-arrow-down\"></span></a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"nav-drop-down\" data-nav-item=\"table\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"overflow-x\" <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F765%2F2%2F139\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/765/2/139</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n                      <P>We report the discovery of a pair of quasars at <I>z</I> = 1.487, with a separation of 8<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />585 \u00b1 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />002. Subaru Telescope infrared imaging reveals the presence of an elliptical and a disk-like galaxy located almost symmetrically between the quasars, in a cross-like configuration. Based on absorption lines in the quasar spectra and the colors of the galaxies, we estimate that both galaxies are located at redshift <I>z</I> = 0.899. This, as well as the similarity of the quasar spectra, suggests that the system is a single quasar multiply imaged by a galaxy group or cluster acting as a gravitational lens, although the possibility of a binary quasar cannot be fully excluded. We show that the gravitational lensing hypothesis implies that these galaxies are not isolated, but must be embedded in a dark matter halo of virial mass ~4 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<SUP>14</SUP> <I>h</I>             <SUP>\u20131</SUP>             <SUB>70</SUB> <I>M</I>             <SUB>\u2609</SUB> assuming a Navarro-Frenk-White model with a concentration parameter of <I>c</I>             <SUB>vir</SUB> = 6, or a singular isothermal sphere profile with a velocity dispersion of ~670\u00a0km\u00a0s<SUP>\u20131</SUP>. We place constraints on the location of the dark matter halo, as well as the velocity dispersions of the galaxies. In addition, we discuss the influence of differential reddening, microlensing, and intrinsic variability on the quasar spectra and broadband photometry.</P>       </div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/765/2/139&doi=10.1088/0004-637X/765/2/139&exportFormat=iopexport_bib&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-bib\" title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/765/2/139&doi=10.1088/0004-637X/765/2/139&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2013ApJ...765..139R\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://nedwww.ipac.caltech.edu/cgi-bin/nph-objsearch?refcode=2013ApJ...765..139R&search_type=Search \" target=\"_blank\">NED Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://simbad.u-strasbg.fr/simbad/sim-ref?querymethod=bib&simbo=on&bibcode=2013ApJ...765..139R\" target=\"_blank\">Simbad Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/0004-637X/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj461723s1\" name=\"apj461723s1\">1.\u00a0INTRODUCTION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Quasar pairs belong to one of three categories: gravitational lenses, binary quasars, and apparent (projected) pairs. Both components of a pair in the first two categories have the same redshift.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apj461723fn2\">11</a></sup> Gravitationally lensed quasars dominate the small separation \u0394\u03b8 <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 3'' quasar pairs. These are produced by galaxy scale lenses, and for the past three decades have proven to be invaluable astrophysical and cosmological probes (e.g., Claeskens &amp; Surdej <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r20\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r20\">2002</a>; Schneider et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r89\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r89\">2006</a>). At larger separations, the lensing probability falls quickly (Turner et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r97\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r97\">1984</a>) and becomes increasingly dominated by environmental effects due to galaxy groups and clusters (e.g., Keeton et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r58\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r58\">2000</a>). The largest separation, double-image lensed quasar confirmed to date is Q0957+561 (\u0394\u03b8 = 6<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />17; Walsh et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r100\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r100\">1979</a>), and the only larger separation lensed quasars known are the five-image SDSS\u00a0J1004+4112 (\u0394\u03b8 = 14<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />7; Inada et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r49\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r49\">2003</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r47\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r47\">2005</a>) and the three-image SDSS\u00a0J1029+2623 (\u0394\u03b8 = 22<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5; Inada et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r48\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r48\">2006</a>; Oguri et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r78\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r78\">2008b</a>). The two latter systems are lensed by clusters. Clearly, a larger sample is needed to observationally study the lensing probability distribution in the large-separation range (e.g., Oguri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r70\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r70\">2006</a>; More et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r65\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r65\">2012</a>). These wide lenses provide unique opportunities to study the interplay between baryonic and dark matter in groups (\u0394\u03b8 <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10''), as well as the dark matter distribution in clusters<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apj461723fn3\">12</a></sup> (e.g., Oguri et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r74\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r74\">2004</a>).</p><p>Most known large-separation quasar pairs, however, are binary quasars (e.g., 19 pairs with 3'' &lt; \u0394\u03b8 &lt; 10'' in the sample of Hennawi et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r37\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r37\">2006</a>). These are physically associated quasars, in the sense that they are either gravitationally interacting or belong to the same group or cluster. Binary quasars are found to be more abundant than predicted from extrapolating the quasar correlation function to small scales (e.g., Djorgovski <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r25\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r25\">1991</a>; Hewett et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r38\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r38\">1998</a>; Hennawi et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r37\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r37\">2006</a>; Kayo &amp; Oguri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r55\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r55\">2012</a>), which may imply that quasar activity is triggered and sustained by tidal interactions or mergers (e.g., Mortlock et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r67\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r67\">1999</a>; Hopkins et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r41\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r41\">2005</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r42\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r42\">2006</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r40\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r40\">2007</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r43\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r43\">2008</a>; Gregorio-Hetem et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r34\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r34\">2009</a> and references therein).</p><p>Many quasar pairs found in the range 3'' <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> \u0394\u03b8 <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10'' have proven difficult to classify as either lensed or binary quasars (e.g., Q2345+007; Weedman et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r101\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r101\">1982</a>). In these systems, no plausible lenses have been identified in the foreground, although the redshifts of the two components are identical and the spectra are quite similar. The spectra of gravitationally lensed images need not necessarily be identical, since they are prone to effects such as delayed intrinsic variability, chromatic microlensing, and differential extinction in the lensing galaxy (e.g., Wucknitz et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r103\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r103\">2003</a>; Yonehara et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r104\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r104\">2008</a>). On the other hand, there is little dynamic range in the spectral variation of quasars, meaning that pairs can have similar spectra by chance (e.g., Mortlock et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r67\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r67\">1999</a>).</p><p>The suggestion of lensing by \"dark\" lenses (e.g., Koopmans et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r60\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r60\">2000</a>) or cosmic strings (Vilenkin <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r99\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r99\">1984</a>) has been put forward for these quasar pairs. There are in fact very few criteria for discriminating between the lensing and binary quasar hypotheses, without monitoring the luminosity variability of the components, in order to look for a time delay. Instead, clues about this population of pairs have been found statistically. For instance, it has been argued that most of these quasar pairs are not lensed quasars, based on comparisons of the optical and radio properties of the population (Kochanek et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r59\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r59\">1999</a>). As for individual pairs, their nature must in general be estimated from the ensemble of their observed properties.</p><p>In this paper, we report the discovery in the course of the Sloan Digital Sky Survey Quasar Lens Search (SQLS; Oguri et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r75\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r75\">2006</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r76\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r76\">2008a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r77\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r77\">2012b</a>; Inada et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r46\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r46\">2008</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r50\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r50\">2010</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r51\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r51\">2012</a>) of a new large-separation (\u0394\u03b8 ~ 8<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5) quasar pair that has proven difficult to classify, SDSS\u00a0J132059.17+164402.59 and SDSS\u00a0J132059.73+164405.6 (hereafter jointly SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644). In the near infrared, we detect two galaxies located between the quasars, producing an apparent cross-like configuration. In Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s2\">2</a>, we give a brief description of the lens candidate selection from the SDSS data, and in Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s3\">3</a> we present our imaging and spectroscopic follow-up observations. Next, we estimate photometric redshifts for the two galaxies and show that they are located in the foreground (Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s4\">4</a>). We subsequently proceed with the gravitational lens mass modeling of this system (Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s5\">5</a>). In Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s6\">6</a>, we examine the quasar spectral and photometric differences, whereas in Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s7\">7</a> we explore the environment of the system. Finally, we summarize our conclusions in Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s8\">8</a>. Throughout this paper, we assume the concordance cosmology with <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> = 70\u2009<em>h</em><sub>70</sub>\u2009km<sup>\u22121</sup>\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>\u2009Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>, \u03a9<sub><em>M</em></sub> = 0.27, and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723ieqn1.gif\" alt=\"$\\Omega _\\Lambda =0.73$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\Omega _\\Lambda =0.73</script></span></span>.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj461723s2\" name=\"apj461723s2\">2.\u00a0DISCOVERY IN THE SLOAN DIGITAL SKY SURVEY</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS-I, 2000\u20132005, SDSS-II, 2005\u20132008; York et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r105\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r105\">2000</a>) is a combination of imaging and spectroscopic surveys which have mapped ~10,000\u00a0deg<sup>2</sup> of the sky, centered at the North Galactic Cap. The observations are made at the Apache Point Observatory in New Mexico, USA, with a dedicated 2.5\u00a0m wide-field telescope (Gunn et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r36\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r36\">2006</a>). The imaging survey is conducted in five broadband filters, centered at 3561(<em>u</em>), 4676 (<em>g</em>), 6176 (<em>r</em>), 7494 (<em>i</em>), and 8873\u00a0\u00c5 (<em>z</em>) (Fukugita et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r32\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r32\">1996</a>; Gunn et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r35\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r35\">1998</a>; Doi et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r26\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r26\">2010</a>). The automated pipeline reduction achieves an astrometric accuracy better than about 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1 (Pier et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r83\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r83\">2003</a>), and a photometric zero-point accuracy better than about 0.01\u00a0mag over the entire survey area, in the <em>g</em>, <em>r</em>, and <em>i</em> bands (Hogg et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r39\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r39\">2001</a>; Smith et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r91\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r91\">2002</a>; Ivezi\u0107 et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r53\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r53\">2004b</a>; Tucker et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r95\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r95\">2006</a>; Padmanabhan et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r79\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r79\">2008</a>). The spectroscopic observations are carried out with a multi-fiber spectrograph between 3800\u20139200\u2009\u00c5, with a resolution of <em>R</em> ~ 1800\u20132100 (Blanton et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r13\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r13\">2003</a>). All of the data have been made publicly available, in periodic data releases (Stoughton et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r92\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r92\">2002</a>; Abazajian et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r1\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r1\">2003</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r2\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r2\">2004</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r3\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r3\">2005</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r4\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r4\">2009</a>; Adelman-McCarthy et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r5\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r5\">2006</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r6\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r6\">2007</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r7\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r7\">2008</a>).</p><p>The SQLS has been one of the most successful strong lens surveys conducted to date. It identifies lensed quasar candidates among spectroscopically confirmed quasars in the SDSS (Richards et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r84\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r84\">2002</a>; Schneider et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r90\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r90\">2010</a>), by combining two selection algorithms, designed to find both small- and large-separation lenses, respectively. The morphological selection identifies small-separation candidates that are unresolved, yet poorly fitted by the point-spread function (PSF); the color selection algorithm searches, in the vicinity of every confirmed quasar, for resolved stellar objects that have similar colors to the quasar. The SQLS has discovered more than 40 new lensed quasars so far (Inada et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r51\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r51\">2012</a>). Together with previously known lenses rediscovered by the SQLS, it has produced a sample of ~60 lensed quasars, roughly half of all of the lensed quasars discovered to date.</p><p>SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644 is a large-separation lensed quasar candidate identified by the color selection algorithm. We reproduce the SDSS multi-band composite image of the system in Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f1\">1</a>. A faint red object, classified by the automatic pipeline as a galaxy, is detected in between the two blue stellar components constituting the lensed quasar candidate, toward North. We give the SDSS photometric results for the quasars and the red object in Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t1\">1</a>. As a result of the finite size of the optical fibers in the SDSS spectrograph, spectra are usually not obtained for two objects less than 55'' apart (the \"fiber collision\"; Blanton et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r13\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r13\">2003</a>), and therefore SDSS spectroscopy is available for only one member of the pair. We reproduce the spectrum of that object, classified as a quasar at <em>z</em> = 1.5024\u2009 \u00b1 \u20090.0024, in Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f2\">2</a> (in blue).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj461723f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj461723f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0SDSS <em>gri</em> color-composite (Lupton et\u00a0al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r63\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r63\">2004</a>) centered on the SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644 pair, obtained on 2005 June 6. rowspan=\"1\">21.96\u2009\u00b1 0.27</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">21.60\u2009\u00b1 0.80</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<strong>Notes.</strong> The AB magnitudes are corrected for Galactic extinction following Schlegel et\u00a0al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r88\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r88\">1998</a>). The object designations are those from Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s3-2\">3.2</a>. The first line for each object gives the PSF magnitudes (A, B), or best model magnitudes (G2), and the second line gives aperture photometry magnitudes. Objects G3 and G5 (see Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f4\">4</a>) were also identified as photometric targets by the SDSS pipeline, but their magnitudes are not reproduced, as they are considered unreliable.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n            Download table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t1_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/765/2/139\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t1_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj461723s3\" name=\"apj461723s3\">3.\u00a0FOLLOW-UP SPECTROSCOPY AND IMAGING</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apj461723s3-1\" name=\"apj461723s3-1\">3.1.\u00a0Spectroscopy of the Quasar Pair</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Follow-up spectroscopy aimed to ensure that both stellar components are quasars at the same redshift, and to assess their spectral similarities, was performed at the Astrophysical Research Consortium Telescope (ARC 3.5\u00a0m), located at the Apache Point Observatory. Spectra were taken with the Dual Imaging Spectrograph, equipped with a combination of blue and red gratings, B400/R300. The observations were conducted on 2009 February 19 with a 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5 width slit oriented so that both stellar components are on the slit. One 1500\u00a0s exposure was taken at an effective airmass of 1.63. The extracted spectral coverage was 3700\u201310000\u2009\u00c5, with a resolution of <em>R</em>\u2009 ~ \u2009500. The data reduction was performed using standard IRAF<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apj461723fn4\">13</a></sup> tasks.</p><p>The spectra shown in Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f2\">2</a> (black and red) indicate that both stellar components have similarly shaped quasar broad emission lines (BELs; C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii]</span>, Mg\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>) at the same wavelengths, and therefore are quasars at the same redshift. Our redshift estimate based on the C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii]</span> and Mg\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> peaks (including a reanalysis of the original SDSS spectrum, which was taken in a longer, 4200\u00a0s exposure, and better resolution <em>R</em>\u2009 ~ 1850\u20132000) is 1.487 \u00b1 0.001, which is slightly lower than, and incompatible with, the value in the SDSS database and its associated error bar. We identify absorption lines in both quasars as the Mg\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> 2796\u2009\u00c5, 2803\u2009\u00c5 doublet and possibly Fe\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> 2599.4\u2009\u00c5, indicating <em>z</em> = 0.899. We identify another Mg\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> doublet at <em>z</em> = 1.168, present only in quasar B (the quasar designation corresponds to that in Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f4\">4</a>).</p><p>The flux ratio A/B increases with wavelength, from ~0.8 to ~1.5. This could be explained by a combination of microlensing, differential extinction and intrinsic variability, or simply differential slit losses, and does not constitute by itself an argument against the gravitational lensing hypothesis. We provide a more thorough comparative analysis of the spectra in Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s6\">6</a>.</p><p>Overall, the identical redshift of the two quasars, the similarity of the shapes of the BELs, as well as the presence of absorption lines at the same redshift, appear consistent with the gravitational lensing hypothesis. Of course, these features are fully consistent with the binary quasar hypothesis as well, in which case spectral differences arise naturally, as there are two distinct quasars.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj461723s3-2\" name=\"apj461723s3-2\">3.2.\u00a0UH88 and Subaru Telescope Imaging Data</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Optical follow-up imaging observations were originally conducted with the Tektronix 2048 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 2048 CCD camera (Tek2k; <em>V</em>, <em>R</em>, <em>I</em>, and <em>z</em> bands) and the Wide Field Grism Spectrograph 2 (WFGS2; <em>z</em> band) at the University of Hawaii 2.2\u00a0m (UH88) telescope, in 2009 April. The Tek2k camera has a 7<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcm.gif\" alt=\"farcm\" align=\"baseline\" />5 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 7<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcm.gif\" alt=\"farcm\" align=\"baseline\" />5 field of view and a pixel scale of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />219\u00a0pixel<sup>\u22121</sup>. The WFGS2 has a 11' <b>&#x00d7;</b> 11' field of view and focal reducer of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />34\u00a0pixel<sup>\u22121</sup>, and was used in imaging mode with the Tek2k camera. Bias and flat frames were also obtained, and the data were reduced using standard IRAF tasks. The standard star system PG0918+029 (Landolt <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r61\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r61\">1992</a>) was observed for photometric calibration, but the zero points determined from the five individual stars have a scatter of ~0.2\u00a0mag, possibly indicative of non-photometric conditions. In order to obtain a better precision, the instrumental magnitudes of 10 bright stars in the field of view were compared to their SDSS <em>ugriz</em> magnitudes. The R. Lupton (2005, unpublished) formulae<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apj461723fn5\">14</a></sup> were used to transform between <em>ugriz</em> and <em>VRI</em> magnitudes, which resulted in smaller scatter. The exposure time, airmass, seeing, zero-point uncertainty, and observation date are given for each band in Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t2\">2</a>.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apj461723t2\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 2.</b>\u00a0Summary of Follow-up Imaging Observations</p><table 2</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<strong>Notes.</strong> Zero-point uncertainties in the <em>V</em>, <em>R</em>, <em>I</em>, and <em>z</em> filters include the scatter between the values obtained from the 10 stars used in the estimation, as well as the Lupton (2005) transformations. In the <em>J</em>, <em>H</em>, and <em>Ks</em> filters, the uncertainties include those quoted in the catalogue and the aperture photometry errors. There, the actual error may be ~0.05\u00a0mag, if the differences between catalogues and the values obtained from different frames of the standard star are included. Among the two <em>z</em>-band observations, only the deeper one is considered throughout this article.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n            Download table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t2_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/765/2/139\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t2_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>In the deeper UH88 images (the <em>I</em> and <em>z</em> filters), in addition to the faint photometric object detected between the two quasars in the SDSS data, another object is detected, located almost symmetrically with respect to the quasars (Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f3\">3</a>). In order to determine the morphological and photometric properties of these two objects, as well as to estimate photometric redshifts, we obtained higher resolution near-infrared (<em>J</em>, <em>H</em>, and <em>Ks</em> bands) images with the Multi-Object InfraRed Camera and Spectrograph (MOIRCS; Ichikawa et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r44\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r44\">2006</a>; Suzuki et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r93\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r93\">2008</a>), at the Subaru 8.2\u00a0m telescope (Iye et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r54\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r54\">2004</a>), in 2010 April. MOIRCS has two HAWAII-2 2048<b>&#x00d7;</b>2048 detectors providing a 3<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcm.gif\" alt=\"farcm\" align=\"baseline\" />94 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 6<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcm.gif\" alt=\"farcm\" align=\"baseline\" />90 total field of view, with a pixel scale of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />117\u00a0pixel<sup>\u22121</sup>. The data (Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t2\">2</a>) were reduced with the MCSRED software package (I. Tanaka et\u00a0al., in preparation), and the standard star FS33 (Leggett et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r62\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r62\">2006</a>) was used to estimate the photometric zero points.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj461723f3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj461723f3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Imaging of SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644 with the UH88 telescope. In each of the <em>V</em>, <em>R</em>, <em>I</em>, and <em>z</em> bands, the images to the left show the original observations, the ones in the center show the best models obtained with GALFIT, and those to the right show the residuals after subtracting the fitted models. The snapshots are 11<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />8 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 16<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />6. North is up and east is to the left.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj461723f3\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/downloadFigure/figure/apj461723f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj461723f3\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj461723f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>In the <em>J</em>, <em>H</em>, and <em>Ks</em> imaging (Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f4\">4</a>), the two faint objects are clearly detected. They have extended morphology and red colors, indicative of fairly high-redshift galaxies. In each band, we modeled all four objects simultaneously using the public software GALFIT (Peng et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r81\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r81\">2002</a>), with nearby bright stars as PSF templates. We fitted the galaxies with a S\u00e9rsic profile convolved with the PSF. The resulting astrometry and photometry (including the UH88 photometry) are given in Tables\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t3\">3</a> and\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t4\">4</a>, respectively. We were able to characterize the morphology of the two galaxies, which we summarize in Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t5\">5</a>. The S\u00e9rsic index of G1 is close to the canonical value for elliptical galaxies, <em>n</em> = 4, whereas for G2 it is close to the typical value for disk galaxies, <em>n</em> = 1. We therefore assume that G1 is an elliptical galaxy, whereas G2 is disk-like. This is also consistent with the bluer color of G2.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj461723f4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj461723f4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Subaru Telescope imaging of SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644. Galactic extinction (Schlegel et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r88\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r88\">1998</a>) and atmospheric extinction have been corrected. Quoted errors are statistical errors only. Errors do not include uncertainties in the photometric zero points (Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t2\">2</a>), in the PSF or due to the galaxy modeling parameters. Aperture photometry has been measured in all bands with a fixed ~3<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1 diameter aperture (~1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />9 for G4 and G5), selected to maximize the amount of light from the target, as well as minimize contamination from the other objects. In the <em>V</em>, <em>R</em>, <em>I</em>, and <em>z</em> bands, contamination may be higher due to the larger seeing. In order for the models to converge, the morphological parameters of the S\u00e9rsic profiles fitted to these four bands were constrained to the values determined in the <em>Ks</em> band (i.e., S\u00e9rsic index fixed to the closest canonical value of 1 or 4, effective radius).\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n            Download table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t4_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/765/2/139\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a id=\"apj461723t5\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 5.</b>\u00a0Morphological Parameters of the Two Bright Galaxies</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj461723t5\" data-toolbar-img=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t5_lr.gif\" data-toolbar-type=\"table\" data-toolbar-title=\"Table 5\"><colgroup><col scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Position Angle (deg)</th>\n</tr>\n</thead><tbody><tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">G1</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" by GALFIT in the <em>Ks</em> band, with attached statistical errors. The position angle is measured from north toward east.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n            Download table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t5_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/765/2/139\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t5_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>We also detected three additional fainter objects in the vicinity of the system. While FWHM measurements and GALFIT modeling are not very reliable, our analysis suggests that the objects are extended, and so we mark them as galaxies G3, G4, and G5 in Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f4\">4</a>. Aperture photometry, summarized in Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t4\">4</a>, indicates that the colors of G4 (but not of G3 and G5) are very similar to those of G1 and G2, and we therefore assume that this is a galaxy at about the same redshift. We also remark that the <em>J</em>\u2013<em>H</em> colors of G3 and G5 are different from those of A and B, and it is therefore unlikely that these are additional, faint gravitationally lensed quasar images.</p><p>In the UH88 and Subaru Telescope imaging observations, the flux ratio A/B is above unity, in opposition to the SDSS results. The chromatic change in the brightness ranking of the quasar images could be explained by microlensing (see Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s6-2\">6.2</a>).</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj461723s4\" name=\"apj461723s4\">4.\u00a0PHOTOMETRIC REDSHIFTS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In order to determine whether the two brighter galaxies are located in the foreground of the quasar pair, as is required by the gravitational lens hypothesis, we need to estimate their redshifts. We estimate photometric redshifts, based on the magnitudes of the galaxies in different filters, using the template-fitting methods implemented in the publicly available HyperZ (Bolzonella et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r15\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r15\">2000</a>) and EAzY (Brammer et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r16\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r16\">2008</a>) algorithms. These algorithms fit the observed magnitudes to spectral energy distribution (SED) templates, via \u03c7<sup>2</sup> minimization. As templates, we employ the observed mean SEDs of local galaxies from Coleman et\u00a0al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r22\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r22\">1980</a>, hereafter CWW), which are extrapolated into the ultraviolet and near-infrared with the evolutionary models of Bruzual &amp; Charlot (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r17\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r17\">1993</a>). The EAzY algorithm also uses the redshift distribution of galaxies of a given apparent magnitude as a Bayesian luminosity prior, following Benitez (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r9\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r9\">2000</a>). This helps to break the degeneracies between multiple probability distribution peaks at different redshifts.</p><p>In order to obtain consistent results for the two algorithms, we had to increase the uncertainties on the magnitudes to about double the values quoted in Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t4\">4</a>, which also makes most of the aperture- and model-determined values consistent. In addition to the statistical errors quoted in that table, additional sources of errors could potentially be introduced by the zero-point uncertainties (Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t2\">2</a>), possible blending of sources due to their close proximity and large seeing, different pixel size in the <em>V</em>-, <em>R</em>-, <em>I</em>-, and <em>z</em>-band observations (aperture photometry), or the modeling difficulties and PSF uncertainties (model magnitudes).</p><p>The results of the photometric redshift estimates are shown in Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f5\">5</a> and Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t6\">6</a>. G1 is well-fitted by an elliptical template, and G2 by an Sbc template, consistent with the expectations from the galaxy morphologies (see Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s3-2\">3.2</a>). Although the probability distributions are rather broad, they are in agreement, inside the 1\u03c3 interval estimated from the redshift probability distributions, with <em>z</em> = 0.899, which is the redshift of the absorption lines identified in both spectra. The redshift of the second Mg\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> absorption system in quasar B, at <em>z</em> = 1.166, is rejected at about the 2\u03c3 confidence level, while the probability of a redshift as high as the quasar redshift appears negligible. Therefore, throughout the rest of this work, we assume that these galaxies are responsible for the absorption lines at <em>z</em> = 0.899.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj461723f5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj461723f5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f5_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f5_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0Photometric redshift probability distributions, normalized to unit area, for the two galaxies G1 (left) and G2 (right). The redshift of the quasar pair, as well as those of the absorption line systems identified in the quasar spectra, are marked with vertical lines.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download (2)</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n            Download table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t6_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/765/2/139\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t6_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>For the faintest objects we detected, G3, G4, and G5, the redshift probability distributions are very broad (Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t6\">6</a>). G3 and G5 show a smaller preferred redshift of <em>z</em> ~ 0.5. On the other hand, G4 has a preferred redshift of 1.11, very close to that of the Mg\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> absorption system at <em>z</em> = 1.168. This absorption system is quite strong, with an equivalent width ~2.9\u2009\u00c5 in the rest frame of the absorber. Here, the projected proper distance between B and G4 is 21\u2009<em>h</em><sup>\u22121</sup><sub>70</sub>\u00a0kpc at <em>z</em> = 1.168. On the other hand, the Mg\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> absorption system at <em>z</em> = 0.899 has equivalent widths ~2.9\u2009\u00c5 and ~4.5\u2009\u00c5 (in the spectra of quasars A and B, respectively) for impact parameter ~35\u2009<em>h</em><sup>\u22121</sup><sub>70</sub>\u00a0kpc, corresponding to the distances between the quasars and G1, G2. Such strong absorptions at small impact parameters are consistent with the literature (e.g., Churchill et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r19\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r19\">2005</a> and references therein). The stronger absorption in quasar B could be explained by its closer proximity to one of the galaxies (G1).</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj461723s5\" name=\"apj461723s5\">5.\u00a0GRAVITATIONAL LENS MASS MODELING</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We next proceed with modeling the system as a gravitational lens. The observational constraints provided by the available astrometry and photometry are the positions of the images A and B, their fluxes (we take the flux ratio to be either ~1.0\u2009 \u00b1 \u20090.2 or ~1.4 \u00b1 0.2, as we will show in Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s6\">6</a>), and the positions of the two main galaxies expected to act as lenses, G1 and G2. We also know the redshift of the source, <em>z</em> = 1.487, and of the lenses, <em>z</em> = 0.899. As the number of observational constraints is limited, we focus on simple singular isothermal sphere (SIS) and Navarro\u2013Frenk\u2013White (NFW) lens models and their elliptical counterparts, with or without shear. The singular isothermal profile is known to be a good approximation for the mass distribution in galaxies (e.g., Rusin &amp; Kochanek <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r86\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r86\">2005</a> and references therein). It has also been employed in the lens modeling of galaxy clusters (e.g., Inada et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r49\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r49\">2003</a>). The NFW profile (Navarro et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r68\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r68\">1996</a>), on the other hand, is frequently employed as an approximation of the mass distribution in dark matter haloes, which dominate galaxy clusters. Constraints can also be placed on the \"strength\" of the lenses, represented (in the case of the SIS model) by their velocity dispersions, which we infer from the observed luminosities. To this end, we estimate the rest frame <em>R</em>-band absolute magnitudes of G1 and G2 as ~ \u2212 22.5 and ~ \u2212 22.3, respectively, calculated with HyperZ from the best-fit E and Sbc templates. We use the Faber\u2013Jackson (F-J; Faber &amp; Jackson <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r28\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r28\">1976</a>) and the Tully\u2013Fisher (T-F; Tully &amp; Fisher <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r96\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r96\">1977</a>) laws, with parameters given in Binney &amp; Tremaine (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r12\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r12\">2008</a>), and infer velocity dispersions of 237 \u00b1 54\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> and 163 \u00b1 15\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>, respectively. For G2, which is disk-like, we have assumed an SIS profile and estimated the velocity dispersion as <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723ieqn2.gif\" alt=\"$\\sigma =v_{\\rm circ}/\\sqrt{2}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sigma =v_{\\rm circ}/\\sqrt{2}</script></span></span>, where <em>v</em><sub>circ</sub> is the circular velocity obtained from the T-F law.</p><p>The velocity dispersion estimate above implies that the brightest galaxy G1 has an Einstein radius of only ~0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5 \u00b1 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2, and an enclosed mass of ~(2.1 \u00b1 1.5) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>11</sup>\u2009<em>h</em><sup>\u22121</sup><sub>70</sub>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>. However, the large separation between A and B (~8<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />6), assuming they are lensed images of a single source, implies an enclosed mass of ~1.2 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup>\u2009<em>h</em><sup>\u22121</sup><sub>70</sub>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>. Consequently, this cannot be a gravitational lens system where the main deflectors are the individual members of a group of galaxies, and we need to invoke a significant embedding dark matter halo.</p><p>A question worth posing is to what extent must the faintest objects G3, G4, and G5, be taken into account when building gravitational lens models. We only estimate the velocity dispersion of G4, assumed to be at <em>z</em> = 0.899 or <em>z</em> = 1.168 (see Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s4\">4</a>). We neglect the contribution of G3 and G5, as they are located further away from images A and B, and are most likely at a significantly different redshift from G1, G2, and G4. Assuming that G4 is located at the same redshift as G1 and G2 leads to velocity dispersions \u03c3 = 150 \u00b1 35\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> or \u03c3 = 105 \u00b1 10\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>, from the F-J and T-F laws, respectively. A smaller effective velocity dispersion would be inferred if placing G4 at redshift <em>z</em> = 1.168 (see Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723app1-1\">A.1</a> for details).</p><p>We subsequently explore gravitational lens models in which the lensing potentials of G1, G2, and G4 are boosted by an embedding dark matter halo. We model the observed galaxies with SIS profiles, and the dark matter halo with either an SIS or an NFW profile. We consider the case where the dark matter halo is centered at the location of the brightest galaxy G1, as well as the case where its position is a free parameter, on which we obtain constraints. We only consider models with 0\u00a0degrees of freedom (d.o.f.), which we are able to fit perfectly (\u03c7<sup>2</sup> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/Lt.gif\" alt=\"Lt\" align=\"absmiddle\" /> 1). This means that for the models where the center of the dark matter halo is fixed, we save two parameters that we can use to introduce a shear/ellipticity and associated position angle. The gravitational lens modeling is performed with <em>glafic</em> (Oguri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r71\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r71\">2010</a>).</p><p>We show the critical lines and caustics for these models in Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f6\">6</a>. We report the best-fitted parameters of the models (velocity dispersion or virial mass of the dark matter halo, ellipticity/shear and associated position angle, total magnification and time delay) in Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t7\">7</a>. The velocity dispersions of G2 and G4 (also G1 in the case of the \"SIS free\" and \"NFW free\" models of Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f6\">6</a>) were fixed at the values inferred earlier, but we also explored, on a suitable grid, combinations of velocity dispersions in the ranges allowed by the F-J and T-F laws. In this case, we only considered models with \u03c7<sup>2</sup> &#x2264; 2.3, corresponding to the 1\u03c3 confidence interval for two parameters. The effect of the range of velocity dispersions is reflected in the range of values for the parameters in Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t7\">7</a>, as well as the allowed position of the dark matter halo, for the \"SIS free\" and \"NFW free\" models.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj461723f6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj461723f6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f6_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f6_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0Gravitational lensing models. Top left: A and B are two images of a source lensed primarily by a large velocity dispersion SIS (with shear) profile centered on G1; top right: same as previous, but the position of the primary SIS (without shear) lens is not fixed; bottom left: A and B are two images of a source lensed primarily by a large elliptical NFW profile centered on G1; bottom right: same as previous, but the position of the spherical NFW lens is not fixed. The critical lines and caustics are drawn in blue (outer curves) and light green (inner curves), respectively, with the velocity dispersions of G1, G2, and G4 fixed at their most probable values (237, 163, and 118\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>, respectively; see Section\u00a0<a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s5\">5</a> and Section\u00a0<a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723app1-2\">A.2</a>), unless the adopted model requires otherwise. Only NFW profiles with <em>c</em><sub>vir</sub> = 6 are drawn. Symbols denote image, source and lens positions (red squares, magenta triangles, and black crosses, respectively). The black regions close to the center of the large caustic of the free position models show the locations of this primary lens, as the velocity dispersions of G1, G2, and G4 are varied over the allowed 1\u03c3 range (\u03c7<sup>2</sup> &#x2264; 2.3).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj461723f6\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/downloadFigure/figure/apj461723f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj461723f6\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj461723f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apj461723t7\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 7.</b>\u00a0Parameters of the Best-fit Lensing Models</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj461723t7\" data-toolbar-img=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t7_lr.gif\" data-toolbar-type=\"table\" data-toolbar-title=\"Table 7\"><colgroup><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col
