rowspan=\"1\">NFW free</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"pm\">(4.9 \u00b1 0.4) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup>\u2009<em>h</em><sup>\u22121</sup><sub>70</sub>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> (<em>c</em><sub>vir</sub> = 30)</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u00a0\u22c5\u22c5\u22c5\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u00a0\u22c5\u22c5\u22c5\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"pm\">41 \u00b1 18</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"pm\">\u2212638 \u00b1 262</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"pm\">(4.0 \u00b1 0.4) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>14</sup>\u2009<em>h</em><sup>\u22121</sup><sub>70</sub>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> (<em>c</em><sub>vir</sub> = 6)</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u00a0\u22c5\u22c5\u22c5\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u00a0\u22c5\u22c5\u22c5\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"pm\">99 \u00b1 46</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"pm\">\u2212547 \u00b1 223</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"pm\">(3.2 \u00b1 0.1) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>15</sup>\u2009<em>h</em><sup>\u22121</sup><sub>70</sub>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> (<em>c</em><sub>vir</sub> = 2)</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u00a0\u22c5\u22c5\u22c5\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u00a0\u22c5\u22c5\u22c5\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"pm\">175 \u00b1 75</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"pm\">\u2212380 \u00b1 80</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<strong>Notes.</strong> The range on each parameter is obtained by changing the velocity dispersions of G1, G2, and G4 over the allowed 1\u03c3 range (see Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s5\">5</a>). <em>e</em> and \u03b3 are the ellipticity and shear, respectively, and \u03b8<sub><em>e</em></sub>, \u03b8<sub>\u03b3</sub> are the respective position angles (measured east of north). \u03bc<sub>tot</sub> is the total magnification of images A and B, and the predicted time delay \u0394<em>t</em> is positive if image A leads.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n            Download table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t7_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/765/2/139\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t7_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>There are two major differences between the NFW and SIS models considered for the dark matter halo. First, we parameterize the NFW profile by its virial mass <em>M</em><sub>vir</sub> and concentration parameter <em>c</em><sub>vir</sub> (as opposed to just a single parameter, \u03c3, for the SIS model), and we fix <em>c</em><sub>vir</sub> at a fiducial value of six, close to the typical value for galaxy clusters at the mass range and redshift we are interested in (e.g., Binney &amp; Tremaine <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r12\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r12\">2008</a> and reference therein). We explore different choices of <em>c</em><sub>vir</sub> in Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723app1-2\">A.2</a>. Second, the NFW profile is shallower than SIS at the center, and can therefore produce an additional faint central image. Therefore, for the \"NFW free\" model, we require that the model produce no more than three images, and that the central image be ~50\u00a0times or more fainter than B, corresponding to our detection limit at signal-to-noise ratio (S/N) ~\u20095 in the deepest band, <em>Ks</em>. The central image cannot be one of the faint objects G3 or G5, as those objects are too far away from the midpoint between A and B, are also most likely extended, and have different colors.</p><p>Finally, in addition to the models where the dark matter halo is fixed on G1, we tried a similar range of SIS+\u03b3 models at the location of G2, but were unable to produce good fits. We note that G2 is farther away from the line connecting images A and B, and therefore a model centered on G2 is expected to do a poorer job of fitting the data. We do not try to fix the dark matter halo at the location of G4, as we are not sure whether this galaxy is at the same redshift as G1 and G2, and we also do not expect the dark matter halo to be centered on a fainter galaxy of the group/cluster.</p><p>Our main result in this section is that any reasonable mass model reproduces the observed image configuration. In order for this system to be a two-image gravitationally lensed quasar, we do however require a massive dark matter halo in which the visible galaxies are embedded. This halo should be centered either on G1 (but not on G2) or at some location close to the midpoint between A and B. The choice of the intrinsic flux ratio (either ~1.0 or ~1.4) does not affect the successful mass models qualitatively. Finally, in Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723app1-3\">A.3</a>, we discuss several caveats regarding the estimated velocity dispersions of the galaxies.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj461723s6\" name=\"apj461723s6\">6.\u00a0QUASAR SPECTRO-PHOTOMETRIC DIFFERENCES</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The three physical phenomena known to modify the spectra of the images of gravitationally lensed quasars are differential extinction due to dust in the lensing galaxy (e.g., Falco et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r29\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r29\">1999</a>), microlensing due to stars in the lens (e.g., Chang &amp; Refsdal <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r18\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r18\">1979</a>; Schneider et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r89\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r89\">2006</a>), and intrinsic quasar variability coupled with the time delays between images. The first process is a static one, whereas the other two are time-dependent. We assess their effects on the continuum and broadband fluxes in turn.</p><h3 id=\"apj461723s6-1\" name=\"apj461723s6-1\">6.1.\u00a0Differential Extinction</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The most striking difference in the follow-up spectra of quasars A and B is the gradual drop in the flux ratio A/B with wavelength, shortward of about 7000\u2009\u00c5. Such an effect has previously been observed in several lensed quasars, such as SDSS\u00a0J1001+5027 (Oguri et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r73\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r73\">2005</a>) and SDSS\u00a0J1313+5151 (Ofek et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r69\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r69\">2007</a>). Differential extinction (reddening) between the quasar images could explain this effect, as it is most effective at short wavelengths. Here, we caution, however, that the monotonous drop in the flux ratio may also be due to unknown differential slit losses in the ARC 3.5\u00a0m spectroscopy. Nonetheless, we do not expect the differential slit losses to be large, because the flux drop is supported by the available photometry from the <em>u</em> to <em>z</em> band, which shows quasar A to be redder than B (see also Figures\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f1\">1</a> and\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f7\">7</a>). In addition, the flux ratios inferred from the UH88 photometry, obtained just two months after the follow-up spectra, are consistent with the spectral ratios at the respective wavelengths. On the other hand, extinction should be weakest in the longest wavelength filter <em>Ks</em>, which corresponds roughly to the <em>J</em> band in the rest frame of G1 and G2. A caveat, however, is that there is no obvious galaxy responsible for the extinction in A, i.e., located much closer to A than the other galaxies are to B.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj461723f7\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj461723f7\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f7_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 7.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 7.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f7_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 7.</strong>\u00a0Broadband photometric flux and flux ratios of quasars A and B, determined from the aperture photometry in Tables\u00a0<a class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t1\">1</a> and\u00a0<a class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t4\">4</a>, which include curve of growth corrections. The photometric bands are marked, and the horizontal bars of the flux ratios indicate the width of the filters. We use the photometric errors corresponding to the zero-point uncertainties, from Table\u00a0<a class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t2\">2</a>. The <em>WISE</em> mid-infrared data from Table\u00a0<a class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t8\">8</a>, which is considered less reliable (see Section\u00a0<a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s6-3\">6.3</a>), is not plotted.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj461723f7\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/downloadFigure/figure/apj461723f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj461723f7\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj461723f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>In contrast, the large change in the spectral slope of quasar A at short wavelengths, on a time span of 13\u00a0months, between the original SDSS and ARC observations, requires a significant contribution of the time-dependent phenomena. While the slope change can also be caused by slit losses, the two sets of photometric data in the <em>V</em>\u2013<em>z</em>-band range, taken ~4\u00a0years apart, also shows the brightness rank of the two quasars interchanging, with A getting brighter, and B becoming fainter. Therefore, time-dependent phenomena must be acting on the quasars. We next look into the extent to which microlensing can explain these data.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj461723s6-2\" name=\"apj461723s6-2\">6.2.\u00a0Microlensing</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We assume that the microlensing variability is due to the motion of stars in the lensing galaxies, which we consider to be dominated by the proper motion of the galaxies, with transverse velocity <em>v</em><sub>\u22a5</sub> ~ \u03c3, where \u03c3 is the velocity dispersion in the galaxy group/cluster. Following Richards et\u00a0al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r85\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r85\">2004</a>) in the following formulae, there are two timescales to the microlensing event. The first is the expected duration of the event, which is the time in which the microlens crosses the source,\n</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj461723fd1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723fd1.gif\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{equation} t_\\mathrm{src}\\sim 4.1\\left(\\frac{R_\\mathrm{src}}{1\\ {\\rm lt-day}}\\right)\\left(\\frac{v_\\perp }{700\\ \\mathrm{km\\ s^{-1}}}\\right)^{-1} \\mathrm{yr}, \\end{equation}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>R</em><sub>src</sub> is the size of the source quasar accretion disk in the rest frame ultraviolet, corresponding to the optical region of the observed spectrum of SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644. It has an estimated extent of a few light-days or less (e.g., Morgan et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r66\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r66\">2008</a>).</p><p>The second timescale is the mean interval between microlensing events, which is crudely estimated as the time needed to cross an Einstein radius,\n</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj461723fd2\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723fd2.gif\" alt=\"Equation (2)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{equation} t_\\mathrm{Ein}\\sim 8.2\\left(\\frac{M}{0.1\\ M_\\odot }\\right)^{1/2}\\left(\\frac{v_\\perp }{700\\ \\mathrm{km\\ s^{-1}}}\\right)^{-1}\\,h_{70}^{1/2}\\ \\mathrm{\\ yr}, \\end{equation}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>with <em>M</em> being the mass of the microlens.</p><p>Although these results are rough estimates, they are close to the timescales on which our data show spectral and photometric variations. Careful analytical considerations of the expected microlensing light curves (e.g., Yonehara et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r104\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r104\">2008</a>), as well as ray-shooting techniques to account for the fact that the source could be microlensed by an ensemble of stars at any particular time (e.g., Kayser et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r56\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r56\">1986</a>), show that microlensing can indeed cause variations in amplitude comparable to, or even larger than we observe in the spectro-photometric data. In standard accretion disk theory (e.g., Peterson <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r82\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r82\">1997</a>), short wavelength emission originates at comparatively smaller distances from the central engine, and so is preferentially affected by microlensing. This is in agreement with the large changes we detect at progressively shorter wavelengths in our two-epoch spectra of quasar A. Conversely, since the near-infrared emission is expected to originate at larger distances from the center than the UV or optical continuum, we expect the longest wavelength band <em>Ks</em> (originating approximately at the <em>z</em> band in the quasar rest frame) to be less affected by microlensing.</p><p>On the other hand, the BEL region is as large as a few light-weeks or light-months (e.g., Clavel et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r21\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r21\">1991</a>), with lower ionization lines originating at larger distances. Thus, we expect that, in particular, the Mg\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> BEL is less prone to microlensing effects. We find that the spectral flux ratio A/B of the two images (Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f2\">2</a>) shows a decrease at the center of the Mg\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> BEL, compared to the surrounding continuum.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apj461723fn6\">15</a></sup> This could be indicative of microlensing affecting the continuum, but not the Mg\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> BEL.</p><p>In principle, an estimate of the intrinsic flux ratio can be obtained from the flux ratio in the continuum-subtracted BELs, since they are less prone to microlensing effects than the continuum fluxes. However, in our case, there is the chance that differential slit losses, although believed to be small (see Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s6-1\">6.1</a>), affect the BELs, and therefore we refrain from doing so.</p><p>One possible argument against microlensing could be that, since the two galaxies are fairly distant in projection from the quasar pair (~35\u2009<em>h</em><sup>\u22121</sup><sub>70</sub>\u00a0kpc), there may be no stars to account for microlensing. We note that evidence of microlensing has previously been found in both SDSS\u00a0J1004+4112 (e.g., Richards et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r85\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r85\">2004</a>) and SDSS\u00a0J1029+2623 (e.g., Oguri et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r78\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r78\">2008b</a>), the two large-separation cluster lenses; in those cases, however, the galaxies believed to host the stars responsible for microlensing are located at slightly smaller projected separations of <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20\u201330\u2009<em>h</em><sup>\u22121</sup><sub>70</sub>\u00a0kpc from the quasar images.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj461723s6-3\" name=\"apj461723s6-3\">6.3.\u00a0Intrinsic Variability</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We estimate the magnitude of intrinsic quasar variability using the structure function (e.g., Yonehara et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r104\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r104\">2008</a> and reference therein)\n</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj461723fd3\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723fd3.gif\" alt=\"Equation (3)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{equation} V=(1+0.024M_i)\\left(\\frac{\\Delta t_{\\rm RF}}{\\lambda _{\\rm RF}}\\right)^{0.3} {\\rm \\ mag.} \\end{equation}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>This predicts the magnitude change for a quasar of <em>i</em> band absolute magnitude <em>M<sub>i</sub></em>, at a given rest frame wavelength \u03bb<sub>RF</sub> in units of\u2009\u00c5, for a rest frame time interval between observations of \u0394<em>t</em><sub>RF</sub> (in\u00a0days). The structure function has been measured from a quasar sample with <em>M<sub>i</sub></em> ~ \u221221 to ~\u2009 \u2212 30 (Vanden Berk et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r98\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r98\">2004</a>), in the rest frame optical/UV, and on a rest frame time range of up to two\u00a0years. We also note that the characteristic timescale for optical variability of quasars is of the order 1 year (Ivezi\u0107 et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r52\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r52\">2004a</a>), which is comparable with the time delay in the source rest frame (0.4\u20132.8\u00a0years), in our best lensing models.</p><p>We use the structure function to check if it can reproduce spectral changes in quasar A of a factor of ~2 in flux (~0.75\u00a0mag), at ~4500\u2009\u00c5 (~1800\u2009\u00c5 rest frame) in the 13\u00a0months (~160\u00a0days rest frame) between the original SDSS and the follow-up spectroscopy. Using the image magnifications we obtain from the lensing models (Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t7\">7</a>), and the fact that the source rest frame <em>i</em> band corresponds to the observed <em>H</em>\u2013<em>Ks</em> bands, we estimate the source quasar absolute brightness to be <em>M<sub>i</sub></em> ~ \u2212 22.0 to ~ \u2212 25.7, depending on the choice of lensing model. Since the structure function is larger for fainter quasars, we use the former value and obtain an upper estimate of \u0394<em>m</em> ~ 0.23 (because this is a magnitude difference, it has the same value in the rest frame as in the magnified, observed-frame). This value is too small to explain the spectral changes at short wavelengths. We note, however, that the structure function might be able to explain the flux changes of ~0.3\u00a0mag (\u0394<em>m</em> = 0.25) in both quasars in the <em>z</em> band in ~4 years, and it may also introduce flux variations of about 0.26\u20130.23\u00a0mag at the observed-frame <em>J</em>\u2013<em>Ks</em> bands during the maximum rest frame time interval of the quasar sample, ~2 years. We point out, however, that the structure function is a statistical average over a sample of quasars, and therefore brightness changes of larger amplitude are not ruled out in individual objects (magnitude differences larger than 0.75 occur in about 1% of quasars; Vanden Berk et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r98\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r98\">2004</a>).</p></div><h3 id=\"apj461723s6-4\" name=\"apj461723s6-4\">6.4.\u00a0Estimates of the Intrinsic Flux Ratio</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Our analysis shows that the spectral shapes of A &amp; B (in the continua) are compatible with the gravitational lensing hypothesis, where the differences are caused by differential extinction, microlensing and intrinsic variability. Microlensing and extinction are more important at short wavelengths than intrinsic variability, and slit losses may also contribute to the changes in the spectrum of A. Regarding the intrinsic flux ratio, all three spectral flux ratios of the two quasars, which we plotted in Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f2\">2</a>, are fairly constant in the range 7200\u20138400\u2009\u00c5, with A(SDSS)/A (the original SDSS spectrum of A divided by the follow-up spectrum of A) fairly close to 1, and A/B, A(SDSS)/B\u2009 ~ \u20091.4. This latter value is in excellent agreement with the flux ratio we measure in the <em>J</em>, <em>H</em>, and <em>Ks</em> bands, where we expect differential extinction and microlensing to be weakest. Together, these results suggest that the intrinsic flux ratio, which would ideally be measured in the far-infrared or radio, is ~1.4 \u00b1 0.2, and all three flux-altering physical factors considered are weak in the 7200\u20138400\u2009\u00c5 spectral range. Here, we chose the error bar on the flux ratio somewhat arbitrarily, slightly larger than the scatter in the flux ratio at 7200\u20138400\u2009\u00c5\u00a0 as well as the <em>J</em>, <em>H</em>, and <em>Ks</em> bands.</p><p>The two quasars were also detected in the mid-infrared by <em>WISE</em> (Wright et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r102\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r102\">2010</a>) at almost the same epoch as the Subaru MOIRCS <em>J</em>, <em>H</em>, and <em>Ks</em> observations. The flux ratio at 3.4, 4.6, and 12\u2009\u03bcm is ~1 (Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t8\">8</a>), but we consider this to be broadly consistent with the value above, because the quasars were detected at quite low S/N\u2009 ~ \u200910\u201320 and the fluxes were measured by deblended profile fits at very poor angular resolution (6<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1, 6<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />4, 6<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5, and 12<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />0 in the four bands, respectively). There is also the possibility of unknown flux contamination from the two bright galaxies. The quasar pair also appears to be radio quiet, based on non-detection in the Faint Images of the Radio Sky at Twenty-cm survey, which is sensitive to &gt;1\u00a0mJy\u00a0beam<sup>\u22121</sup>.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apj461723t8\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 8.</b>\u00a0WISE Mid-infrared Photometry of the SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644 Quasar Pair</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj461723t8\" data-toolbar-img=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t8_lr.gif\" data-toolbar-type=\"table\" data-toolbar-title=\"Table 8\"><colgroup><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col></colgroup><thead>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">Object &amp; Flux Ratio</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">W1 (3.4\u2009\u03bcm)</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">W2 (4.6\u2009\u03bcm)</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">W3 (12\u2009\u03bcm)</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">W4 (22\u2009\u03bcm)</th>\n</tr>\n</thead><tbody><tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">A</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">15.32 \u00b1 0.05</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">13.99 \u00b1 0.05</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">10.92 \u00b1 0.10</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">9.06 \u00b1 0.53</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">B</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">15.32 \u00b1 0.05</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">14.04 \u00b1 0.05</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">10.85 \u00b1 0.09</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">7.97 \u00b1 0.20</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">A/B</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">1.00 \u00b1 0.07</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">1.05 \u00b1 0.07</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">0.94 \u00b1 0.12</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">0.53 \u00b1 0.19</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<strong>Notes.</strong> The deblended profile-fit magnitudes are in the Vega system. The observations were performed between 2010 January 7 and 2010 June 30. The values in W4 are particularly unreliable, as the quasars are detected at S/N ~ 2\u20135, with an angular resolution of 12<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />0, larger than the quasar separation.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n            Download table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t8_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/765/2/139\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/suppdata/apj461723t8_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>In view of the <em>WISE</em> data, we also consider the possibility that the intrinsic flux ratio is ~1.0 \u00b1 0.2 (here, we have broadened the scatter in the values measured at 3.4, 4.6, and 12\u2009\u03bcm). This requires that the flux ratio of ~1.4 in the continuum, found in the data redward of the Mg\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> BEL, be due to microlensing. This assumption is still in agreement with the results in Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s6-2\">6.2</a>, and in particular Equation\u00a0(<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apj461723fd1\">1</a>), which is an order-of-magnitude estimate. In this interpretation, the flux change at long wavelengths occurs on timescales larger than those sampled by our observations, whereas those at shorter wavelengths are noticeable. This assumption is testable via deeper, high resolution mid-infrared observations, as the mid-infrared emitting region is expected to be too large for microlensing to occur. This interpretation would also explain why the flux ratio decreases slightly with increasing wavelength between the <em>H</em> and <em>Ks</em> bands, reaching unity in the <em>WISE</em> bands.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj461723s7\" name=\"apj461723s7\">7.\u00a0ENVIRONMENT OF THE LENS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Since our attempts to model the system as a two-image gravitationally lensed quasar require a significant additional dark matter component, we next look at the environment of the lens in order to search for clues to the existence of a possible galaxy cluster.</p><p>We proceed by making a color\u2013color diagram of the galaxies in the MOIRCS field of view (~1900 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 1660\u2009<em>h</em><sup>\u22121</sup><sub>70</sub>\u00a0kpc at <em>z</em> = 0.899, centered on SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644), using the <em>J</em>, <em>H</em>, and <em>Ks</em> bands. We aim to isolate the galaxies that are similar in color to G1 and G2, and study their spatial distribution around the target. We use SExtractor (Bertin &amp; Arnouts <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r11\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r11\">1996</a>) to catalogue and match all of the objects in the field of view, and we isolate the galaxies by requiring the SExtractor stellarity parameter to be\u00a0less than 0.2 in all three bands. We eliminate a few galaxies that are very close together so that aperture photometry would fail, which leads to a sample of 98 galaxies.</p><p>From Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t4\">4</a>, using the aperture photometry, we determine the colors (<em>J</em>\u2009\u2212\u2009<em>H</em>, <em>H</em>\u2009\u2212\u2009<em>Ks</em>)\u00a0= (0.87 \u00b1 0.07, 0.88 \u00b1 0.05) for G1, and (0.87 \u00b1 0.08, 0.80 \u00b1 0.05) for G2. In color\u2013color space, G1 and G2 lie close to each other, and close to the concentration at (<em>J</em>\u2009\u2212\u2009<em>H</em>, <em>H</em>\u2009\u2212\u2009<em>Ks</em>) ~ (0.9, 0.7). Thus, there may be an excess of galaxies at the redshift of G1 and G2, indicative of a cluster.</p><p>We attempt a color cut at 0.8 &lt;<em>J</em> \u2212 <em>H</em> &lt; 1.1 and 0.6\u00a0&lt;<em>H</em> \u2212 <em>Ks</em> &lt; 0.9, which includes the concentration of galaxies as well as G1 and G2. In Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f8\">8</a> (right), we plot the positions of the galaxies selected by this color-cut from the MOIRCS field of view centered on SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644; they seem to spatially surround the target. We caution, however, that the broad color cut may contain galaxies at different redshift. A multidimensional color plot would help break the degeneracies, but the UH88 bands, even the deep <em>z</em> band, detect only a few of the galaxies visible in the MOIRCS bands and do not allow a systematic treatment.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj461723f8\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj461723f8\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f8_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 8.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 8.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f8_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 8.</strong>\u00a0Color\u2013color (left) and spatial distribution diagrams (right) of the galaxies in the Subaru MOIRCS field of view. The colors and associated error bars are based on aperture photometry (MAG<span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723ieqn3.gif\" alt=\"$\\_$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\_</script></span></span>APERT) determined with SExtractor. The color-cut region used to isolate galaxies possibly associated with the candidate cluster at <em>z</em> ~ 0.9 is marked with dashed lines. The spatial distribution diagram identifies the galaxies whose colors match the color-cut region. We use the field of view of only one of the MOIRCS detectors, centered on the target. East is up and north is to the right. 100\u00a0arcsec at <em>z</em> = 0.9 corresponds to a proper scale of 790 <em>h</em><sup>\u22121</sup><sub>70</sub>\u00a0kpc.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj461723f8\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/downloadFigure/figure/apj461723f8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj461723f8\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj461723f8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Our analysis therefore hints that G1 and G2 are embedded at the center of a galaxy cluster, which in turn supports the existence of a heavy dark matter halo associated with the cluster, as required by our models in Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s5\">5</a>. In Appendix\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723app2\">B</a>, we provide an estimate of the content of dark matter.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj461723s8\" name=\"apj461723s8\">8.\u00a0SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We have reported the discovery of SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644, a pair of quasars at <em>z</em> = 1.487 with two galaxies in between, in a cross-like configuration, as part of the SQLS. We have estimated the redshift of the two galaxies as <em>z</em> = 0.899, based on absorption lines observed in the quasar spectra and consistent with the photometric redshifts of the galaxies.</p><p>We used the observational constraints to investigate whether the system is a two-image gravitationally lensed quasar or a binary quasar. We showed that the key to differentiating between the gravitational lens and binary pair hypotheses is whether or not there exists a dark matter halo at the location of the system that is capable of boosting the gravitational potential of the galaxies. We estimated the required virial mass or velocity dispersion of this halo, as well as the expected time delays and image magnifications, using a variety of SIS and NFW profiles. We were also able to show that the halo can only be centered on the brighter, elliptical galaxy G1 or somewhere in between the two quasars (but not on the disk-like galaxy G2), as well as to set constraints on the velocity dispersions of these two galaxies, and an additional, fainter third galaxy. The third galaxy may be located at either <em>z</em> = 0.899 or <em>z</em> = 1.168. In the first case, it would further support the existence of a halo indicative of a galaxy group or cluster, and in the second it would explain the presence of a strong absorption system in one of the quasars.</p><p>We have found a substantial number of galaxies in the field, surrounding the quasar pair, with colors consistent with <em>z</em> = 0.899 (based, however, on only three filters). If this is a galaxy cluster, then the quasar pair would reside behind the center of the cluster potential, which would provide the required dark matter halo lens. The calculated mass-to-light ratio, assuming the lensing hypothesis (Appendix\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723app2\">B</a>), is in agreement with what would be expected from a cluster-scale lens.</p><p>We have performed a comparative analysis of the spectra of the two quasars (as well as inferred the intrinsic flux ratio of the two quasars), and concluded that all of the observed differences can be attributed to a combination of extinction, microlensing, and intrinsic variability, in support of the gravitational lensing hypothesis, with the microlensing being dominant.</p><p>The most similar known system to our target is arguably Q0957+561 (Walsh et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r100\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r100\">1979</a>), which has two quasar images separated by 6<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />17 and one lensing galaxy that is part of a confirmed cluster that boosts the image separation. That system shows, in addition, evidence of strongly lensed arcs, which are the extended images of the lensed quasar host galaxy (Bernstein et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r10\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r10\">1993</a>).</p><p>We conclude that SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644 is a probable gravitationally lensed quasar, although we are unable to prove this beyond a doubt based on our available data. We propose the following observations as a means of establishing the true nature of this object unambiguously.\n</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">As the most time-effective way of testing the lensing hypothesis, we suggest deep adaptive optics or <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> observations to study the morphology of the quasar host galaxies, and look for extended arc-like features, characteristic of gravitational lensing. We note that there is a 16.6\u00a0mag (<em>R</em> band) star ~58<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />4 from the target, which could be used as a tip-tilt star for the Laser Guide Star Adaptive Optics capabilities of the Subaru Telescope.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Deep, wide field optical imaging of the region could be used to detect weak lensing associated with the possible lensing cluster.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Deep X-ray observations would test the presence of the galaxy group/cluster by providing an independent measure of the velocity dispersion of intracluster gas, as well as determining its dynamical center.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">4.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Spectroscopy of the two brightest galaxies would determine if they are indeed at the same redshift, consistent with the spectral absorption lines, as assumed in our galaxy cluster hypothesis. These galaxies are faint in the <em>V</em>, <em>R</em>, <em>I</em>, <em>z</em>, and SDSS bands, and therefore the photometric redshifts are fairly uncertain. Multi-object spectroscopy would provide a means to check the redshift of the surrounding galaxies, and therefore either consistently prove or disprove the cluster hypothesis. At the very least, deeper imaging in other bands is required to constrain the photometric redshift of the surrounding galaxies.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">5.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Long-time monitoring of the quasar fluxes to look for correlated variations (a time delay) would convincingly reveal the nature of this system, as long as the correlated variations are not suppressed by microlensing. Although we do not possess sufficient constraints to estimate a robust time delay, our best estimates are ~1\u20137 years, based on our lensing models.</div></li></ul><p>Should this system indeed prove to be a gravitational lens, then it would be the third-largest separation gravitationally lensed quasar in the SQLS, and the largest separation two-image lensed quasar known. These large separation lenses are important to include in a complete statistical sample, such as the one provided by the SQLS (Inada et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r51\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r51\">2012</a>), in order to constrain the hierarchical structure formation at cluster mass scales. They are also exceedingly rare, because the probability of quasars strongly lensed by clusters is 1\u20132 orders of magnitudes smaller than that of quasars lensed by galaxies (e.g., Inada et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r51\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r51\">2012</a>).</p></div>\n<div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>This work was supported in part by the FIRST program \"Subaru Measurements of Images and Redshifts (SuMIRe),\" World Premier International Research Center Initiative (WPI Initiative), MEXT, Japan, and Grant-in-Aid 23740161 and 24740171 for Scientific Research from the Japan Society for the Promotion of Science (JSPS).</p><p>The authors thank the anonymous referee for criticism that helped improve the paper. C.E.R. and I.K. acknowledge the support of the JSPS Research Fellowship. D.S. acknowledges supports from Deutsche Forschungsgemeinschaft, reference SL172/1-1. M.A.S. acknowledges support from NSF grant AST-0707266. The authors recognize and acknowledge the very significant cultural role and reverence that the summit of Mauna Kea has always had within the indigenous Hawaiian community. We are most fortunate to have the opportunity to conduct observations from this superb mountain.</p><p>This publication makes use of data products from the Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer, which is a joint project of the University of California, Los Angeles, and the Jet Propulsion Laboratory/California Institute of Technology, funded by the National Aeronautics and Space Administration.</p><p>Funding for the SDSS and SDSS-II has been provided by the Alfred P. Sloan Foundation, the Participating Institutions, the National Science Foundation, the U.S. Department of Energy, the National Aeronautics and Space Administration, the Japanese Monbukagakusho, the Max Planck Society, and the Higher Education Funding Council for England. The SDSS Web site is <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"url_link\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"http://www.sdss.org/\">http://www.sdss.org/</a>.</p><p>The SDSS is managed by the Astrophysical Research Consortium for the Participating Institutions. The Participating Institutions are the American Museum of Natural History, Astrophysical Institute Potsdam, University of Basel, Cambridge University, Case Western Reserve University, University of Chicago, Drexel University, Fermilab, the Institute for Advanced Study, the Japan Participation Group, Johns Hopkins University, the Joint Institute for Nuclear Astrophysics, the Kavli Institute for Particle Astrophysics and Cosmology, the Korean Scientist Group, the Chinese Academy of Sciences (LAMOST), Los Alamos National Laboratory, the Max-Planck-Institute for Astronomy (MPIA), the Max-Planck-Institute for Astrophysics (MPA), New Mexico State University, Ohio State University, University of Pittsburgh, University of Portsmouth, Princeton University, the United States Naval Observatory, and the University of Washington.</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj461723app1\" name=\"apj461723app1\">APPENDIX A: ADDITIONAL CONSIDERATIONS ON THE LENS MODELING</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apj461723app1-1\" name=\"apj461723app1-1\">A.1.\u00a0A Lens Model for G4</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>By considering that G4 is located at <em>z</em> = 1.168 and applying the F-J and T-F laws, the velocity dispersion of this galaxy is \u03c3 = 175 \u00b1 40\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> or 125 \u00b1 12\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>, respectively. Assuming that the effect of G4 on the lensing configuration is small, we can treat G4 as an SIS lens situated at <em>z</em> = 0.899, but with a smaller effective velocity dispersion. This eliminates the need to introduce multiple lens planes and is justified by the fact that the convergence from G4 at B (arbitrarily taken as the object closest to G4, but projected to <em>z</em> = 1.168) is as small as \u03ba ~ \u03b8<sub>Ein</sub>/2<em>d</em><sub><em>B</em> \u2212 <em>G</em>4</sub> ~ 0.028 \u00b1 0.012 (F-J) or ~0.014 \u00b1 0.002 (T-F), where \u03b8<sub>Ein</sub> is the Einstein radius of the lens G4 estimated from the velocity dispersion (assuming an SIS profile) and <em>d</em><sub><em>B</em> \u2212 <em>G</em>4</sub> = 2<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />49 is the projected angular distance between B and G4. From Keeton et\u00a0al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r57\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r57\">2003</a>), using \u03ba (convergence)\u00a0= \u03b3 (shear) for the SIS profile, the effective convergence of G4 at <em>z</em> = 0.899 is \u03ba<sub>eff</sub> = (1 \u2212 \u03b2)\u03ba/(1 \u2212 2\u03b2\u03ba) ~ 0.012\u2009 \u00b1 \u20090.005 (F-J), or ~0.006\u2009 \u00b1 \u20090.002 (T-F), where \u03b2 = <em>D</em><sub>12</sub><em>D</em><sub>OS</sub>/(<em>D</em><sub>O2</sub><em>D</em><sub>1S</sub>), <em>D<sub>ij</sub></em> = <em>D</em>(<em>z<sub>i</sub></em>, <em>z<sub>j</sub></em>) are angular diameter distances <em>z</em><sub>1</sub> = 0.899, <em>z</em><sub>2</sub> = 1.168, and O, S refer to the observer and source, respectively. The effective convergence corresponds to an SIS profile at G4 with effective velocity dispersion ~81 \u00b1 19\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> (F-J) or ~57 \u00b1 5\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> (T-F). We conclude that, considering all possibilities, an SIS model at <em>z</em> = 0.899 for G4 would have a velocity dispersion in the 1\u03c3 range of ~118 \u00b1 66\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj461723app1-2\" name=\"apj461723app1-2\">A.2.\u00a0Dependence on <em>c</em><sub>vir</sub></h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Here, we investigate how the choice of the concentration parameter <em>c</em><sub>vir</sub> of the NFW profiles affects our results in Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s5\">5</a>. We explore <em>c</em><sub>vir</sub> in the range from 2 to 30, and we optimize all of the other model parameters. Increasing <em>c</em><sub>vir</sub> has the effect of decreasing the total magnification of the images, and increasing the time delay (Table\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj461723t7\">7</a>). We plot the resulting <em>c</em><sub>vir</sub>\u2013virial mass relation in Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f9\">9</a>. We obtain virtually the same degeneracy for the free NFW profile as for the one fixed at G2. For comparison, we also plot some recently published results on the <em>c</em><sub>vir</sub>\u2013virial mass relation: the results of <em>N</em> \u2212body simulations by Duffy et\u00a0al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r27\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r27\">2008</a>), as well as the theoretical and observational results inferred by Oguri et\u00a0al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r72\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r72\">2012a</a>) from fitting lensing clusters at <em>z</em> ~ 0.45. The strong lens selected sample behind the Oguri et\u00a0al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r72\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r72\">2012a</a>) relations is known to be biased toward overestimating the concentrations due to halo triaxiality. If SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644 is indeed a lens system, then it should include the same bias, as it is selected as a strong lens. All of these relations are observed to intersect the SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644 curves.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj461723f9\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj461723f9\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f9_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 9.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 9.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f9_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 9.</strong>\u00a0The <em>c</em><sub>vir</sub>\u2013virial mass degeneracy of the NFW profiles. The thick black lines show published relations from both simulations and observations, while the thin lines show the corresponding 1\u03c3 ranges. A caveat is that the Oguri et\u00a0al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r72\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r72\">2012a</a>) theoretical curve has been derived for a cluster redshift <em>z</em> = 0.45, which corresponds to the mean redshift of their observed cluster sample. The blue and red curves show the results of these work, for the NFW profile fixed at G1, as well as with unconstrained (free) location, respectively.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj461723f9\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/downloadFigure/figure/apj461723f9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj461723f9\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj461723f9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apj461723app1-3\" name=\"apj461723app1-3\">A.3.\u00a0Caveats on the Velocity Dispersions of the Galaxies</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We discuss two caveats. A first caveat is that when estimating the range of velocity dispersion for each galaxy in Section\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723s5\">5</a>, we have not taken into account the possible evolution of the T-F and F-J with redshift. Although there is no consensus in the literature in this regard (e.g., B\u00f6hm et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r14\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r14\">2004</a> &amp; F\u00e9rnandez Lorenzo <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r31\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r31\">2010</a> for T-F, Rusin et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r87\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r87\">2003</a> &amp; Treu et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r94\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r94\">2006</a> for F-J), the tendency is that galaxies had smaller rotational velocities and velocity dispersions in the past for the same luminosity. This would reduce even more the contribution of the galaxies to the mass models dominated by the dark matter halo.</p><p>A second caveat applies to G4, in the case that this galaxy is located at <em>z</em> = 1.168. In this case, G4 would also be lensed by the dark matter halo at <em>z</em> = 0.899, and therefore magnified by a factor of ~1.5\u20133, depending on the assumed lensing model. This would mean that the galaxy is intrinsically fainter, and its contribution to the mass models would be further reduced. On the other hand, the undeflected position of G4 would be closer to the main deflector, increasing its influence. We have checked that the best-fit models considered in this section (from which we exclude the contribution of G4) would not produce multiple images of this galaxy (<em>z</em> = 1.168 being closer to the redshift of the main lens than <em>z</em> = 1.487, the critical surface density for multiple images is increased).</p></div></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj461723app2\" name=\"apj461723app2\">APPENDIX B: MASS-TO-LIGHT RATIOS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Here, we compute the mass-to-light (<em>M</em>/<em>L</em>) ratio by assuming that SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644 is a lensed system, and compare it with the <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> ratio of the other lenses discovered by the SQLS<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apj461723fn7\">16</a></sup> (Inada et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r51\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r51\">2012</a>). We fit each lens with an SIS profile, in which case the Einstein radius \u03b8<sub><em>E</em></sub> is simply half the image separation. For the quasars with more than two images, we consider \u03b8<sub><em>E</em></sub> to be half the largest separation between the images. The mass inside the Einstein radius is simply determined as\n</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj461723fd4\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723fd4.gif\" alt=\"Equation (B1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray} M(\\leqslant \\theta _E) &=& \\frac{D_\\mathrm{OL}D_\\mathrm{OS}}{D_\\mathrm{LS}}\\frac{c^2\\theta _E^2}{4G}\\simeq 1.23 \\times 10^8\\ \\,h_{70}^{-1}\\,M_\\odot \\nonumber\\\\ && \\times \\left(\\frac{D_\\mathrm{OL}D_\\mathrm{OS}/D_\\mathrm{LS}}{1\\,h_{70}^{-1}\\ \\mbox{Mpc}}\\right)\\ \\left(\\frac{\\theta _\\mathrm{E}}{1\\ \\mbox{arcsec}}\\right)^2. \\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\tB1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>To determine the luminosity of the SQLS lenses, we referred to the discovery papers (Inada et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r51\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r51\">2012</a> and references therein) and estimated the rest frame <em>R</em>-band magnitudes of all of the galaxies with available photometry, located between the images, using the HyperZ algorithm. To convert the rest frame magnitudes into luminosities, we used <em>M<sub>R</sub></em> = 4.48\u20132.5log\u2009<em>L</em>/<em>L</em><sub>\u2609, <em>R</em></sub>, where <em>M</em><sub>\u2609, <em>R</em></sub> = 4.48 is the <em>R</em>-band magnitude of the Sun. The <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> ratios were computed in terms of the solar <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> ratio, <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/Upsi.gif\" alt=\"Upsilon\" align=\"bottom\" /><sub>\u2609</sub>\u00a0= 1\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>/<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub>.</p><p>We attached no error bars to the results of these rough calculations. Not accounting for the effects of lens ellipticity and external shear for each (mostly double-image) system should introduce an error of ~10% on the masses (Rusin &amp; Kochanek <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r86\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r86\">2005</a>). Also, the redshifts of several lenses are not accurately known, which influences both the masses and the inferred luminosities. Photometry for most lenses is only available in three bands (we eliminate 10 objects for which the lens is detected in only one band), and their morphology is typically unknown. It is therefore difficult to choose the appropriate CWW spectral template for calculating the rest frame <em>R</em>-band luminosity. However, we know that the majority of lens galaxies are ellipticals, as these have larger velocity dispersions (Turner et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r97\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r97\">1984</a>), and so we generally assume the elliptical template, unless the lens is known to be a spiral, or the fit of another template is much better. Also, luminosities should be more accurate for lenses at <em>z</em> ~ 0.2\u20130.3 because at this redshift the rest frame <em>R</em> band is redshifted into the observed-frame <em>I</em> band, where observations are usually available. For SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644, the lens redshift estimate means that the rest frame <em>R</em> band is redshifted into the <em>J</em> band, where photometry is available.</p><p>The results of our <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> estimates are shown in Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f10\">10</a>, in which we plot the mass-to-light ratios against the reduced image separations. The reduced image separation \u0394\u03b8<sub>red</sub> \u2261 \u0394\u03b8\u2009<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/centerdot.gif\" alt=\"centerdot\" align=\"absmiddle\" /> <em>D</em><sub>OS</sub>/<em>D</em><sub>LS</sub> scales out the dependence on the source and lens distance, thus representing a physical property of the lensing object.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj461723f10\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj461723f10\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f10_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 10.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 10.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/765/2/139/Full/apj461723f10_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 10.</strong>\u00a0Mass-to-light ratios in the rest frame <em>R</em> band for 52 SQLS gravitationally lensed quasars, as well as for SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644 (assuming it is also a lensed quasar). Here \u0394\u03b8<sub>red</sub> is the reduced image separation (see Appendix\u00a0<a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj461723app2\">B</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj461723f10\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/downloadFigure/figure/apj461723f10\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj461723f10\" href=\"/0004-637X/765/2/139/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj461723f10\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The image separation is the most important observable, because it reflects the depth of the gravitational potential of the lensing object and therefore the structure responsible for the lensing phenomenon. Since clusters are dominated by dark matter much more than normal galaxies, we expect that as the image separation increases from the galaxy lens to the cluster lens range, so does the <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> ratio. Accordingly, the result we obtain in Figure\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj461723f10\">10</a> shows a positive correlation between the image separation and <em>M</em>/<em>L</em>. Most small-separation lenses at \u0394\u03b8 ~ 1'' are clustered at <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> ~ 1\u201310\u2009<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/Upsi.gif\" alt=\"Upsilon\" align=\"bottom\" /><sub>\u2609</sub>, consistent with the estimates from the dynamics of elliptical galaxies and their stellar populations (Padmanabhan et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r80\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r80\">2004</a>), whereas the two known cluster-scale lenses SDSS\u00a0J1004+4112 and SDSS\u00a0J1029+2623 both have a larger <em>M</em>/<em>L</em>. We obtain a similarly large <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> for SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644.</p></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 id=\"footnotes\">Footnotes</h2>\n\t\t<div data-mobile-collapse=\"\">\n\t\t\t<ul class=\"clear-list wd-content-footnotes\">\n\t\t\t\t<li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apj461723fn1\" id=\"apj461723fn1\">*\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Based on data collected at Subaru Telescope, which is operated by the National Astronomical Observatory of Japan. Use of the UH2.2\u00a0m telescope for the observations is supported by NAOJ.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apj461723fn2\" id=\"apj461723fn2\">11\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Small redshift differences in the components of binary quasar pairs, such as 661 \u00b1 173\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> for LBQS 0015+0239 (Impey et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r45\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r45\">2002</a>), are consistent with the line-of-sight velocity difference of bound pairs of galaxies.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apj461723fn3\" id=\"apj461723fn3\">12\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>It is worth mentioning that in addition to lensed quasars, there are also many known lensed galaxies, with a large range of image separations, which we do not address here (e.g., Faure et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r30\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r30\">2008</a>; Auger et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r8\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r8\">2009</a>; More et\u00a0al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj461723r65\" id=\"fnref-apj461723r65\">2012</a>).</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apj461723fn4\" id=\"apj461723fn4\">13\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The interactive Reduction and Analysis Facility (IRAF) is distributed by the National <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">THE QUASAR-GALAXY CROSS SDSS\u00a0J1320+1644: A PROBABLE LARGE-SEPARATION LENSED QUASAR<SUP>*</SUP>       </h1>\n\n<p class=\"mb-0\">\n\t<span data-authors=\"\">\n\t\t<span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Cristian E. <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F765%2F2%2F139\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/765/2/139</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n                      <P>We report the discovery of a pair of quasars at <I>z</I> = 1.487, with a separation of 8<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />585 \u00b1 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />002. Subaru Telescope infrared imaging reveals the presence of an elliptical and a disk-like galaxy located almost symmetrically between the quasars, in a cross-like configuration. Based on absorption lines in the quasar spectra and the colors of the galaxies, we estimate that both galaxies are located at redshift <I>z</I> = 0.899. This, as well as the similarity of the quasar spectra, suggests that the system is a single quasar multiply imaged by a galaxy group or cluster acting as a gravitational lens, although the possibility of a binary quasar cannot be fully excluded. We show that the gravitational lensing hypothesis implies that these galaxies are not isolated, but must be embedded in a dark matter halo of virial mass ~4 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<SUP>14</SUP> <I>h</I>             <SUP>\u20131</SUP>             <SUB>70</SUB> <I>M</I>             <SUB>\u2609</SUB> assuming a Navarro-Frenk-White model with a concentration parameter of <I>c</I>             <SUB>vir</SUB> = 6, or a singular isothermal sphere profile with a velocity dispersion of ~670\u00a0km\u00a0s<SUP>\u20131</SUP>. We place constraints on the location of the dark matter halo, as well as the velocity dispersions of the galaxies. In addition, we discuss the influence of differential reddening, microlensing, and intrinsic variability on the quasar spectra and broadband photometry.</P>       </div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a
